Identifying Key Biographical Details of Ibn Hisham
Biographical notice of Ibn Hisham with full name, expertise, works, and death details.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract and organize the key biographical details (name, lineage, expertise, major works, death year) of Ibn Hisham from a classical biographical notice.

Core Idea
A scholar's biography verifies the reliability of transmitted knowledge.

We often encounter references to scholars like Ibn Hisham in our study of Islamic texts. Knowing who they are, their specializations, and their works helps us assess the authority of the information they transmit. This lesson will walk you through the essential components of a classical biographical entry, using Ibn Hisham as our case study.

Guided Path
1
Identify the full name and lineage
Read the Arabic text and find the complete name: Abu Muhammad Abdul Malik ibn Hisham ibn Ayyub. Note the multiple lineage and tribal identifiers (al-Himyari, al-Dhuhli, etc.).
These names often include tribe, region, and family connections.
2
List the scholar's areas of expertise
Extract the terms describing his fields: historian (إخباري), genealogist (نسابة), literary scholar (أديب), linguist (لغوي), grammarian (نحوي).
This shows he was a multi-disciplinary scholar.
3
Locate the year and place of death
Find the phrase 'توفي بمصر سنة: 213 هـ'. This gives the death year (213 AH) and location (Egypt).
Death dates help establish a scholar's era and potential teachers/students.
4
Record his major works
Look for the phrase 'من آثاره' followed by the list: Tahdhib al-Sira, explanation of obscure words from sira poems, and Kitab al-Tijan.
These works show his contribution to Islamic history and literature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو أبو محمد عبد الملك بن هشام بن أيوب الحميري ،الذهلي السدوسي، المعافري، البصري، إخباري، نسابة أديب لغوي، نحوي، توفي بمصر سنة : 213 هـ، من آثاره : تهذيب السيرة النبوية شرح ما وقع في أشعار السير من الغريب كتاب التيجان لمعرفة ملوك الزمان، ترجمته في : وفيات الأعيان لابن خلكان ج: ص : 429 .10، ج: .3 ص، 177 : : 2 ص : (223 المؤلفين، ج : - معجم سير أعلام النبلاء للذهبي،
He is Abu Muhammad Abdul Malik ibn Hisham ibn Ayyub al-Himyari, al-Dhuhli, al-Sadusi, al-Ma'afiri, al-Basri, a historian, genealogist, literary scholar, linguist, grammarian, died in Egypt in the year 213 AH. His works include: Tahdhib al-Sira al-Nabawiyya (Refinement of the Prophetic Biography), explanation of the obscure words occurring in the poems of the sira, Kitab al-Tijan li-Ma'rifat Muluk al-Zaman (Book of Crowns for Knowledge of the Kings of Time). His biography is in: Wafayat al-A'yan by Ibn Khallikan, vol. 1, p. 429; vol. 3, p. 177; vol. 2, p. 223; Mu'jam al-Mu'allifin; Siyar A'lam al-Nubala' by al-Dhahabi.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a biographical note providing key details about Ibn Hisham, the author of the famous Sira (biography of the Prophet) referenced in the previous narrative. It lists his full name, lineage, tribal affiliations, his city (Basra), and his specializations: historian, genealogist, literary scholar, linguist, grammarian. It mentions his death year (213 AH/828 CE) and his major works, particularly 'Tahdhib al-Sira' (an edited version of Ibn Ishaq's Sira) and a book on ancient kings. It also gives references to later biographical dictionaries where he is mentioned, such as Ibn Khallikan's Wafayat al-A'yan and al-Dhahabi's Siyar A'lam al-Nubala'.

Key Terms

سيرة
توفي

Checkpoint

A) Writing a commentary on the Quran.
B) Editing and refining the Prophet's biography (Sira).

Key Takeaway
Biographical notices in Islamic texts are essential for verifying a scholar's identity, expertise, and reliability in transmitting knowledge.

Jadal in the Sahaba Era: The Saqifa Debate
Author describes the first major jadal among Muslims after the Prophet's death concerning the caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe how jadal functioned as a constructive debate method in the Sahaba era, using the Saqifa debate as an example.

Core Idea
Jadal was the first tool the Sahaba used to solve unprecedented problems after the Prophet's death.

In this lesson, you will read about the first major jadal among Muslims after the Prophet's death. The Sahaba used structured debate to resolve the critical issue of succession, showing that jadal can be a constructive intellectual tool.

Guided Path
1
Understand the historical context
After the Prophet's death, Muslims faced a new reality without a leader. Why did they need to engage in jadal? The passage states that jadal became an intellectual methodology for solving problems.
The loss of the Prophet created an urgent need for consensus on leadership.
2
Identify the three positions in the debate
List the three groups: the Ansar, the Muhajirun (led by Abu Bakr), and the supporters of 'Ali from Banu Hashim. For each, note the basis of their claim as mentioned in the passage.
The Ansar based their claim on shelter and support; the Muhajirun on precedence; the third group on 'Ali's merits.
3
Analyze the resolution
How did the debate end? The passage says 'the middle group overcame the other two' and the masses followed. Recognize that this was a peaceful resolution through dialectical dialogue, not conflict.
Jadal led to consensus without prolonged strife.
4
Reflect on the constructive role of jadal
Consider why the author presents this example positively. Jadal is shown as a legitimate method for settling differences, contrary to negative perceptions of debate.
This early use of jadal set a precedent for later Islamic legal and theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع
الجدل في عصر الصحابة والتابعين
المطلب الأول : الجدل في عصر الصحابة
أخذ الجدل مكانته كمنهج فكري لحل المشكلات التي كانت تطرأ على المجتمع الإسلامي في عصر الصحابة رضي الله عنهم، ذلك أنه بعد وفاة النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام، وجد المسلمون أنفسهم أمام واقع جديد لم يعرفوه من قبل، ولم يكن يخطر حتى على تصوراتهم وخواطرهم، وأبرز ما هذا الواقع ؛ افتقادهم إلى القائد الذي كانوا يلجئون إليه ويحتكمون إلى أمره ونهيه وهو النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام فكان لابد لهم أن يختاروا لهم قائدا يسير شئونهم ويكون رمزا لدولتهم . أ - الجدل في الخلافة :
وكانت هذه أول مسألة تثير الجدل فيما بينهم، بل لقد كادت تفضي بهم إلى الخصام والنزاع، فقد شهدت سقيفة بني ساعدة أول حوار جدلي بين المسلمين بعد وفاة النبي ، وكان موضوع الحوار يدور حول من يخلف النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام، وما هي الشروط التي ينبغي أن تتوفر في هذا الخليفة حتى يشغل هذا المنصب .
فقد رأى الأنصار أن الخليفة يبغي أن يكون منهم، لما لهم من فضيلة الإيواء والنصرة، ولأنهم هم حماة الإسلام و نصراء الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم والدعاة إليه، ولم يروا أن النبي خص خلافته ببطن من بطون العرب، ولا بقبيلة من قبائلهم، وفريق آخر على رأسهم أبو بكر ، رأوا أن الأمر للمهاجرين وفريق ثالث جعلوها في بني هاشم ونادوا بعلي لامتيازه على كل بني هاشم بالسابقة في الإسلام والدفاع عنه في المواقف الجلى، والعلم والفقه في الدين، ولم يدم الخلاف طويلا، فإن الفريق الوسط قد غلب الفريقين وتبعه جماهير المسلمين
The Fourth Section: Jadal in the Era of the Companions and Followers. First Requirement: Jadal in the Era of the Companions. Jadal took its place as an intellectual methodology for solving problems that arose in the Islamic society in the era of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them. That is because after the death of the Prophet, peace be upon him, the Muslims found themselves facing a new reality they had not known before, nor had it even occurred to their minds and thoughts. The most prominent aspect of this reality was their loss of the leader to whom they used to turn and refer to his command and prohibition, namely the Prophet, peace be upon him. So it was necessary for them to choose a leader to manage their affairs and be a symbol of their state. A - Jadal concerning the Caliphate: This was the first issue that provoked jadal among them; indeed, it almost led them to quarrel and conflict. The Saqifa of Banu Sa'ida witnessed the first dialectical dialogue among the Muslims after the death of the Prophet. The topic of the dialogue revolved around who would succeed the Prophet, peace be upon him, and what conditions should be met in this caliph in order to occupy this position. The Ansar were of the opinion that the caliph should be from among them, due to their virtue of providing shelter and support, and because they were the protectors of Islam, the helpers of the Messenger of Allah, and the callers to it. They did not see that the Prophet had specified his caliphate to any clan of the Arabs or any tribe of their tribes. Another group, headed by Abu Bakr, saw that the matter belonged to the Muhajirun. A third group made it to Banu Hashim and called for 'Ali due to his distinction over all of Banu Hashim in priority in Islam and defense of it in critical situations, and in knowledge and jurisprudence in religion. The disagreement did not last long, for the middle group overcame the other two, and the masses of Muslims followed them.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents a historical account of jadal in the early Islamic period, specifically focusing on the first major debate after the Prophet's death: the Saqifa incident. The author explains that jadal became an essential intellectual tool for resolving new problems. After the Prophet's death, the Muslims faced the unprecedented challenge of choosing a successor. Three positions emerged: the Ansar claimed the caliphate for themselves based on their support for Islam; the Muhajirun, led by Abu Bakr, argued that the caliph should be from the early emigrants; and a third group proposed 'Ali from Banu Hashim. The debate was resolved when the majority accepted Abu Bakr's leadership. This example illustrates how jadal functioned as a practical method for consensus-building in the early community.

Key Terms
الجدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the first major issue that sparked jadal among the Sahaba after the Prophet's death?
A) The collection of the Qur'an
B) The caliphate succession
C) The rules of zakat
D) The direction of prayer

Key Takeaway
The Saqifa debate demonstrates that jadal, when used constructively, can resolve critical issues without conflict.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Method of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib: Adherence to Medinan Consensus
Describes Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib's reliance on Medinan traditions and consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key components of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib's methodology: his reliance on Medinan consensus, his compilation of early fatwas, and his critical examination of traditions.

Core Idea
Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib built his fiqh on the bedrock of Medinan consensus.

Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib was a towering figure in the early Medinan school of jurisprudence. This lesson examines how he collected and evaluated the traditions of the Companions and the early caliphs, and why he never departed from the consensus of Medinan scholars. Understanding his method reveals the textual and consensual foundations of one of the major early legal traditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sources Sa'id relied on
Read the passage and list the Companions and caliphs whose fatwas and judgments Sa'id compiled. This includes Abu Bakr, 'Umar, 'Uthman, 'Ali (before the caliphate), 'A'isha, Ibn 'Abbas, Ibn 'Umar, Zayd ibn Thabit, and Abu Hurayra. Note that he relied heavily on the musnad of Abu Hurayra and the cases of the judges of Medina.
This shows the breadth of his textual foundation.
2
Understand his methodology of examination
The passage mentions that Sa'id examined these traditions with 'nazar i'tibar wa tafattush wa tahqiiq wa tatbiq' (consideration, investigation, verification, and application). Reflect on what each of these terms implies: he did not simply accept reports but critically evaluated them before applying them.
This method distinguishes him from mere transmitters.
3
Analyze his adherence to consensus
The passage states that whatever was unanimously agreed upon among the scholars of Medina, Sa'id and his companions adhered to it resolutely and did not surpass it. Consider why consensus (ijma') was so authoritative for the Medinan school.
This emphasis on consensus later became a key principle in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجدل، الشريفين أثبت الناس في الحديث والفقه، ولذلك جمع فتاوى أبي بكر وعمر وعثمان وأحكامهم، وفتاوى علي قبل الخلافة وعائشة وابن عباس وابن عمر وزيد بن ثابت وأبي هريرة، وقد اعتمد ابن المسيب مسند أبي هريرة كثيرا وقضايا قضاة المدينة وحفظ من ذلك شيئًا كثيرًا، ونظر فيها نظر اعتبار وتفتيش وتحقيق وتطبيق، فما كان مجمعًا عليه بين علماء المدينة عض عليه بالنواجذ هو وأصحابه لا يتجاوزونه (1) . ومن الجانب الآخر في العراق كان إبراهيم النخعي(2) وأصحابه يرون أن عبد الله بن مسعود أثبت في الفقه ... كما أخذ إبراهيم بفتاوى علي وأحكامه مدة خلافته بالكوفة، وأبي موسى الأشعري، وسعد بن أبي وقاص، وقضايا شريح، إذ كان يستشير فيها عمر وعثمان، فعمل إبراهيم في آثار هؤلاء مثلما عمل سعيد في آثار أهل المدينة، وخرج على فقههم بالقياس والاستنباط فيما لم ينصوا فيه واتخذ قضاياهم أصلا له فكان سعيد بن المسيب لسان فقهاء المدينة والمخطط لبنائهم، وكان إبراهيم لسان العراقيين والمؤسس لمذهبهم»(3)
The two noble ones (Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib and Ibrahim al-Nakha'i) were the most established people in hadith and fiqh. Therefore, Sa'id compiled the fatwas of Abu Bakr, 'Umar, 'Uthman and their judgments, and the fatwas of 'Ali before the caliphate, 'A'isha, Ibn 'Abbas, Ibn 'Umar, Zayd ibn Thabit, and Abu Hurayra. Ibn al-Musayyib relied heavily on the musnad of Abu Hurayra and the cases of the judges of Medina, and preserved much of that, and examined them with consideration, investigation, verification, and application. Whatever was unanimously agreed upon among the scholars of Medina, he and his companions adhered to it resolutely and did not surpass it. On the other side in Iraq, Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and his companions held that 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud was more established in fiqh... Likewise, Ibrahim took the fatwas of 'Ali and his judgments during his caliphate in Kufa, and (the fatwas of) Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, and the cases of Shurayh, since he used to consult 'Umar and 'Uthman. So Ibrahim worked with the traditions of these people just as Sa'id worked with the traditions of the people of Medina, and derived (rulings) from their fiqh by analogy and deduction in matters they did not explicitly address, and took their cases as a basis for himself. Thus Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib was the tongue of the fuqahā’ of Medina and the planner of their structure, and Ibrahim was the tongue of the Iraqis and the founder of their school.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage introduces two foundational figures in early Islamic legal methodology. Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib, representing the Medinan school, is portrayed as a strict adherent to the consensus of the Medinan scholars, relying heavily on transmitted traditions and the fatwas of early caliphs and companions. He is methodical in evaluation. On the other hand, Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, representing the Iraqi school, places great emphasis on the fiqh of 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud and the rulings of 'Ali and other Kufan authorities. Importantly, Ibrahim employs qiyas (analogical reasoning) and istinbat (deduction) when explicit texts are lacking. He uses the cases of earlier judges as foundations. The passage highlights the methodological contrast: the Medinan school is more bound to transmitted consensus and direct reports, while the Iraqi school is more open to rational inference and systematic deduction. This sets the stage for later dialectical debates between the two schools.

Key Terms
أثبت
نظر اعتبار وتفتيش وتحقيق وتطبيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the ultimate authority for Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib in determining legal rulings?
A) The personal opinion of the caliph
B) The unanimous agreement of the Medinan scholars
C) The fatwas of 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud
D) Analogical reasoning (qiyas)

Key Takeaway
Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib's methodology was built on careful compilation, critical examination, and strict adherence to the consensus of Medinan scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Method of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i: Qiyas and Istinbat
Describes Ibrahim al-Nakha'i's reliance on Iraqi authorities and his use of analogical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Ibrahim al-Nakha'i used qiyas (analogy) and istinbat (deduction) to extend rulings beyond the explicit texts, and identify the authorities he relied on.

Core Idea
Ibrahim al-Nakha'i liberated fiqh by applying qiyas to the traditions of the Iraqi school.

Ibrahim al-Nakha'i was the leading figure of the early Iraqi school, known for his reliance on 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud and the Kufan authorities. This lesson focuses on his use of qiyas and istinbat to derive rulings where the transmitted traditions were silent. His approach represents a more rationalistic strand in early Islamic legal thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibrahim's preferred authorities
According to the passage, Ibrahim held that 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud was 'more established in fiqh.' He also took the fatwas of 'Ali during his caliphate in Kufa, as well as those of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, and the cases of Shurayh. Note that Shurayh often consulted 'Umar and 'Uthman.
These authorities formed the basis of the Iraqi legal tradition.
2
Understand his method of qiyas and istinbat
The passage says Ibrahim 'derived (rulings) from their fiqh by analogy and deduction in matters they did not explicitly address' (خرج على فقههم بالقياس والاستنباط فيما لم ينصوا فيه). This means he used analogical reasoning (qiyas) and systematic deduction (istinbat) to extend their rulings to new cases.
This is a key difference from Sa'id's method.
3
Contrast with Sa'id's approach
While Sa'id adhered strictly to the Medinan consensus and did not go beyond it, Ibrahim used the transmitted cases as a basis for further reasoning. Consider the implications: Ibrahim's method allowed for greater flexibility and expansion of fiqh.
This contrast later fueled debates between the two schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجدل، الشريفين أثبت الناس في الحديث والفقه، ولذلك جمع فتاوى أبي بكر وعمر وعثمان وأحكامهم، وفتاوى علي قبل الخلافة وعائشة وابن عباس وابن عمر وزيد بن ثابت وأبي هريرة، وقد اعتمد ابن المسيب مسند أبي هريرة كثيرا وقضايا قضاة المدينة وحفظ من ذلك شيئًا كثيرًا، ونظر فيها نظر اعتبار وتفتيش وتحقيق وتطبيق، فما كان مجمعًا عليه بين علماء المدينة عض عليه بالنواجذ هو وأصحابه لا يتجاوزونه (1) . ومن الجانب الآخر في العراق كان إبراهيم النخعي(2) وأصحابه يرون أن عبد الله بن مسعود أثبت في الفقه ... كما أخذ إبراهيم بفتاوى علي وأحكامه مدة خلافته بالكوفة، وأبي موسى الأشعري، وسعد بن أبي وقاص، وقضايا شريح، إذ كان يستشير فيها عمر وعثمان، فعمل إبراهيم في آثار هؤلاء مثلما عمل سعيد في آثار أهل المدينة، وخرج على فقههم بالقياس والاستنباط فيما لم ينصوا فيه واتخذ قضاياهم أصلا له فكان سعيد بن المسيب لسان فقهاء المدينة والمخطط لبنائهم، وكان إبراهيم لسان العراقيين والمؤسس لمذهبهم»(3)
The two noble ones (Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib and Ibrahim al-Nakha'i) were the most established people in hadith and fiqh. Therefore, Sa'id compiled the fatwas of Abu Bakr, 'Umar, 'Uthman and their judgments, and the fatwas of 'Ali before the caliphate, 'A'isha, Ibn 'Abbas, Ibn 'Umar, Zayd ibn Thabit, and Abu Hurayra. Ibn al-Musayyib relied heavily on the musnad of Abu Hurayra and the cases of the judges of Medina, and preserved much of that, and examined them with consideration, investigation, verification, and application. Whatever was unanimously agreed upon among the scholars of Medina, he and his companions adhered to it resolutely and did not surpass it. On the other side in Iraq, Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and his companions held that 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud was more established in fiqh... Likewise, Ibrahim took the fatwas of 'Ali and his judgments during his caliphate in Kufa, and (the fatwas of) Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, and the cases of Shurayh, since he used to consult 'Umar and 'Uthman. So Ibrahim worked with the traditions of these people just as Sa'id worked with the traditions of the people of Medina, and derived (rulings) from their fiqh by analogy and deduction in matters they did not explicitly address, and took their cases as a basis for himself. Thus Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib was the tongue of the fuqahā’ of Medina and the planner of their structure, and Ibrahim was the tongue of the Iraqis and the founder of their school.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage introduces two foundational figures in early Islamic legal methodology. Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib, representing the Medinan school, is portrayed as a strict adherent to the consensus of the Medinan scholars, relying heavily on transmitted traditions and the fatwas of early caliphs and companions. He is methodical in evaluation. On the other hand, Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, representing the Iraqi school, places great emphasis on the fiqh of 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud and the rulings of 'Ali and other Kufan authorities. Importantly, Ibrahim employs qiyas (analogical reasoning) and istinbat (deduction) when explicit texts are lacking. He uses the cases of earlier judges as foundations. The passage highlights the methodological contrast: the Medinan school is more bound to transmitted consensus and direct reports, while the Iraqi school is more open to rational inference and systematic deduction. This sets the stage for later dialectical debates between the two schools.

Key Terms

Contrasting the Medinan and Iraqi Schools: Text vs. Reason
Contrasts the methods of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib and Ibrahim al-Nakha'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare and contrast the methodological priorities of the Medinan school (represented by Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib) and the Iraqi school (represented by Ibrahim al-Nakha'i) in terms of their sources and use of reasoning.

Core Idea
The Medinan school prioritized transmitted consensus; the Iraqi school prioritized analogical deduction.

This passage presents a clear contrast between two foundational early legal schools. The Medinan school, led by Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib, was anchored in the transmitted traditions and consensus of the Medinan scholars. The Iraqi school, led by Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, while also relying on traditions, actively used analogical reasoning to expand the law. Understanding this contrast is essential for appreciating later debates in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core authorities for each school
Sa'id relied on the fatwas of early caliphs and companions from Medina, especially the Medinan consensus. Ibrahim relied on the fiqh of 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud and the Kufan authorities, including 'Ali and Shurayh.
These different authorities reflect regional differences in the availability of Companions.
2
Contrast their use of consensus vs. qiyas
Sa'id never went beyond the unanimous agreement of Medinan scholars. Ibrahim, on the other hand, used qiyas and istinbat to derive new rulings where his authorities had not spoken. The passage says Ibrahim 'took their cases as a basis' and then reasoned from them.
This is the sharpest methodological difference.
3
Evaluate the implications for legal development
The Medinan method produced a fiqh that was conservative and closely tied to the actual practice of the early community. The Iraqi method allowed for more systematic expansion and adaptation to new circumstances. Both approaches have strengths and weaknesses.
This contrast is still discussed in usul al-fiqh today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجدل، الشريفين أثبت الناس في الحديث والفقه، ولذلك جمع فتاوى أبي بكر وعمر وعثمان وأحكامهم، وفتاوى علي قبل الخلافة وعائشة وابن عباس وابن عمر وزيد بن ثابت وأبي هريرة، وقد اعتمد ابن المسيب مسند أبي هريرة كثيرا وقضايا قضاة المدينة وحفظ من ذلك شيئًا كثيرًا، ونظر فيها نظر اعتبار وتفتيش وتحقيق وتطبيق، فما كان مجمعًا عليه بين علماء المدينة عض عليه بالنواجذ هو وأصحابه لا يتجاوزونه (1) . ومن الجانب الآخر في العراق كان إبراهيم النخعي(2) وأصحابه يرون أن عبد الله بن مسعود أثبت في الفقه ... كما أخذ إبراهيم بفتاوى علي وأحكامه مدة خلافته بالكوفة، وأبي موسى الأشعري، وسعد بن أبي وقاص، وقضايا شريح، إذ كان يستشير فيها عمر وعثمان، فعمل إبراهيم في آثار هؤلاء مثلما عمل سعيد في آثار أهل المدينة، وخرج على فقههم بالقياس والاستنباط فيما لم ينصوا فيه واتخذ قضاياهم أصلا له فكان سعيد بن المسيب لسان فقهاء المدينة والمخطط لبنائهم، وكان إبراهيم لسان العراقيين والمؤسس لمذهبهم»(3)
The two noble ones (Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib and Ibrahim al-Nakha'i) were the most established people in hadith and fiqh. Therefore, Sa'id compiled the fatwas of Abu Bakr, 'Umar, 'Uthman and their judgments, and the fatwas of 'Ali before the caliphate, 'A'isha, Ibn 'Abbas, Ibn 'Umar, Zayd ibn Thabit, and Abu Hurayra. Ibn al-Musayyib relied heavily on the musnad of Abu Hurayra and the cases of the judges of Medina, and preserved much of that, and examined them with consideration, investigation, verification, and application. Whatever was unanimously agreed upon among the scholars of Medina, he and his companions adhered to it resolutely and did not surpass it. On the other side in Iraq, Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and his companions held that 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud was more established in fiqh... Likewise, Ibrahim took the fatwas of 'Ali and his judgments during his caliphate in Kufa, and (the fatwas of) Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, and the cases of Shurayh, since he used to consult 'Umar and 'Uthman. So Ibrahim worked with the traditions of these people just as Sa'id worked with the traditions of the people of Medina, and derived (rulings) from their fiqh by analogy and deduction in matters they did not explicitly address, and took their cases as a basis for himself. Thus Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib was the tongue of the fuqahā’ of Medina and the planner of their structure, and Ibrahim was the tongue of the Iraqis and the founder of their school.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage introduces two foundational figures in early Islamic legal methodology. Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib, representing the Medinan school, is portrayed as a strict adherent to the consensus of the Medinan scholars, relying heavily on transmitted traditions and the fatwas of early caliphs and companions. He is methodical in evaluation. On the other hand, Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, representing the Iraqi school, places great emphasis on the fiqh of 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud and the rulings of 'Ali and other Kufan authorities. Importantly, Ibrahim employs qiyas (analogical reasoning) and istinbat (deduction) when explicit texts are lacking. He uses the cases of earlier judges as foundations. The passage highlights the methodological contrast: the Medinan school is more bound to transmitted consensus and direct reports, while the Iraqi school is more open to rational inference and systematic deduction. This sets the stage for later dialectical debates between the two schools.

Key Terms
أثبت
نظر اعتبار وتفتيش وتحقيق وتطبيق
القياس والاستنباط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Sa'id only used hadith from Abu Hurayra, while Ibrahim used hadith from 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud.
B) Sa'id adhered strictly to Medinan consensus and did not go beyond it, while Ibrahim used qiyas and istinbat to extend rulings.
C) Sa'id was from Medina and Ibrahim was from Iraq.
D) Sa'id compiled fatwas and Ibrahim did not.

Key Takeaway
The Medinan and Iraqi schools differed fundamentally in their methodology: the former emphasized transmitted consensus, the latter employed analogical reasoning to expand the law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate, a Medinan jurist argues: 'We follow the consensus of Medina because it is based on the living practice of the Companions.' How might an Iraqi jurist respond, using Ibrahim's method as a basis?
?
Write a short paragraph comparing Sa'id's 'nazar i'tibar...' method with Ibrahim's 'qiyas wa istinbat' method, highlighting their different attitudes toward transmitted texts.

The Mujtahid Imams and Their Inherited Methodology
Historical introduction to jadal in the age of mujtahid imams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the followers of the Successors preserved and applied the prophetic methodology in deriving rulings, setting the stage for jadal among the mujtahid imams.

Core Idea
The mujtahid imams inherited not just knowledge, but a living methodology of debate from the Prophet.

In the previous lesson, we saw how the Successors (tābi‘ūn) developed diverse opinions influenced by their environments. Now we move to the next generation: the followers of the Successors (atbā‘ al-tābi‘īn) and the great mujtahid imams. This passage introduces a new section of the book, explaining what this generation inherited and how that inheritance shaped the practice of jadal (dialectical debate) among them. Understanding this historical background is essential for grasping why jadal was not merely a foreign import but an organic development within Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three inheritances
Read the first sentence carefully. The author lists three things inherited by the followers of the Successors: (1) knowledge of revelation (‛ilm al-waḥy), (2) the fiqh of the Companions and Successors, and (3) the methodology (manhaj) of the Prophet. Note that the methodology is given equal weight to the transmitted knowledge.
The methodology includes principles of debate and reasoning, which later developed into formal jadal.
2
Understand the reason for diversity of schools
The author argues that diversity among the Successors—due to different environments and schools—naturally led to diversity among their students and the schools that followed. This is a causal chain: environment → diversity of opinion → diversity of schools. The quotation from Ibn Hazm lists some of the early prominent jurists as examples of this diversity.
Ibn Hazm’s list includes Abu Hanifa, Sufyan al-Thawri, and Ibn Abi Layla, representing different regional schools.
3
Connect the inheritance to jadal
Reflect on why the author mentions this inheritance and diversity in a book about jadal. The methodology inherited from the Prophet included guidelines for debate and argumentation. The diversity of schools made jadal necessary for resolving disagreements. Thus, this historical context shows that jadal was an integral part of fiqh from the earliest generations, not a later Greek influence.
Jadal was used by the imams to defend their positions and critique others, based on shared methodological principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس
الجدل في عصر الأئمة المجتهدين
ورث أتباع التابعين علم الوحي الذي نقله إليهم الصحابة والتابعون، وورثوا فقه الصحابة والتابعين كما ورثوا أيضًا المنهج الذي بينه الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم وسار عليه الصحابة والتابعون، وقد فقه كثير من فقهائهم هذا المنهج وساروا عليه في تعرفهم على أحكام الوقائع والنوازل (1) . وبما أننا عرفنا أن التابعين قد تنوعت آراؤهم بتنوع بيئاتهم ومدارسهم، فمن الطبيعي أن تتنوع تبعًا لذلك اتجاهات تلاميذهم ومدارسهم، وفي ذلك يقول الإمام ابن حزم الظاهري رحمه «ثم أتى بعد التابعين فقهاء الأمصار كأبي حنيفة (3) وسفيان (4) وابن أبي ليلى()
The Fifth Section
Jadal in the Age of the Mujtahid Imams
The followers of the Successors inherited the knowledge of revelation that the Companions and Successors transmitted to them, and they inherited the jurisprudence of the Companions and Successors, as they also inherited the methodology that the Messenger (peace be upon him) explained and upon which the Companions and Successors proceeded. Many of their jurists understood this methodology and proceeded upon it in their recognition of the rulings of events and incidents (1). And since we know that the Successors had diverse opinions due to the diversity of their environments and schools, it is natural that the directions of their students and schools would vary accordingly. In this regard, Imam Ibn Hazm al-Zahiri (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'Then after the Successors came the jurists of the regions, such as Abu Hanifa (3), Sufyan (4), and Ibn Abi Layla (5) ...'
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section of the book: 'Jadal in the Age of the Mujtahid Imams.' It provides a historical context by explaining that the generation after the Successors (the followers of the Successors, or tābi‘ū al-tābi‘īn) inherited not only the text of revelation and the fiqh (understanding) of the previous generations, but also the methodology (manhaj) for deriving rulings. This methodology was taught by the Prophet and practiced by the Companions and Successors. Many of the later jurists masterfully applied this manhaj in deriving legal rulings for new events. Then the author notes a natural consequence: the Successors themselves had diverse opinions due to different environments and schools, so it is inevitable that their students and the schools that followed them would also reflect that diversity. To support this, he quotes Ibn Hazm, who lists prominent early jurists: Abu Hanifa, Sufyan al-Thawri, and Ibn Abi Layla, among others. This historical overview sets the stage for discussing how jadal (dialectical debate) functioned in that era.

Key Terms
الجدل
أئمة مجتهدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Knowledge of revelation, fiqh of the Companions and Successors, and the methodology of the Prophet.
C) Only the opinions of the Successors.
D) The art of debate from the Greeks.

Key Takeaway
The mujtahid imams inherited the Prophet's methodology, which provided a foundation for jadal as a tool to manage the natural diversity of schools.

The Mujtahid Imams and the Spread of Jadal
Author lists major scholars of the Mujtahid era and their geographic distribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the major jurists of the Mujtahid era and explain how geographic diversity shaped the development of jadal.

Core Idea
The diversity of legal schools arose from regional scholarly traditions, each rooted in local tabi'un teachings.

The era following the Tabi'un saw the rise of great mujtahids who systematized Islamic law. This passage from Ibn Hazm maps the geographical distribution of these scholars, illustrating how jadal (dialectical reasoning) naturally diversified as each region developed its own legal tradition.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Major Scholars
Identify the names mentioned by Ibn Hazm and the cities associated with each. Write them down as a list for reference.
2
Understand the Transmission Chain
Notice that each scholar learned from the Tabi'un of his own region. This localized transmission shaped their methodologies.
3
Recognize the Ijtihad Principle
Scholars used their own reasoning (ijtihad) for matters not found in local traditions. This led to diverse rulings.
4
Connect Diversity to Jadal
Regional differences in methodology naturally led to varied styles of debate (jadal). This diversity enriched Islamic legal theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس
الجدل في عصر الأئمة المجتهدين
ورث أتباع التابعين علم الوحي الذي نقله إليهم الصحابة والتابعون، وورثوا فقه الصحابة والتابعين كما ورثوا أيضًا المنهج الذي بينه الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم وسار عليه الصحابة والتابعون، وقد فقه كثير من فقهائهم هذا المنهج وساروا عليه في تعرفهم على أحكام الوقائع والنوازل (1) . وبما أننا عرفنا أن التابعين قد تنوعت آراؤهم بتنوع بيئاتهم ومدارسهم، فمن الطبيعي أن تتنوع تبعًا لذلك اتجاهات تلاميذهم ومدارسهم، وفي ذلك يقول الإمام ابن حزم الظاهري رحمه (2) الله : «ثم أتى بعد التابعين فقهاء الأمصار كأبي حنيفة (3) وسفيان (4) وابن أبي ليلى (5) بالكوفة، وابن جريج بمكة، ومالك وابن الماجشون (3) بالمدينة، وعثمان وسوار (4) بالبصرة، والأوزاعي (6) بالشام ، والليث بمصر (7)، فجروا على تلك الطريقة من أخذ كل واحد عن التابعين من أهل بلده فيما كان عندهم واجتهادهم فيما لم يجدوا عندهم وهو موجود عند غيرهم لذلك كان هذا العهد امتدادًا للعهد الذي سبقه، حيث إن الأحداث التي شهدتها عهد التابعين وجدت تطورها وامتدادها في هذا العهد.
Fifth Section: Dialectics in the Era of the Mujtahid Imams. The followers of the successors inherited the knowledge of revelation transmitted to them by the Companions and Successors, and they inherited the jurisprudence of the Companions and Successors as well as the methodology that the Prophet (peace be upon him) clarified and upon which the Companions and Successors proceeded. Many of their jurists understood this methodology and proceeded upon it in their determination of rulings regarding events and new cases (1). Since we have known that the Successors varied in their opinions due to the variation of their environments and schools, it is natural that the directions of their students and schools varied accordingly. In this regard, Imam Ibn Hazm al-Zahiri (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'Then after the Successors came the jurists of the regions, such as Abu Hanifa (3), Sufyan (4), and Ibn Abi Layla (5) in Kufa, Ibn Jurayj in Mecca, Malik and Ibn al-Majishun (3) in Medina, Uthman and Sawwar (4) in Basra, al-Awza'i (6) in Sham, and al-Layth in Egypt (7). They proceeded upon that method of each taking from the Successors of his own town what they had and using their own ijtihad regarding matters they did not find with them but which existed with others. Therefore, this era was an extension of the preceding era, for the events witnessed in the era of the Successors found their development and extension in this era.'
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage provides a historical overview of the development of jadal (dialectical reasoning) during the era of the Mujtahid Imams. It begins by stating that the successors of the Tabi'un (followers of the Companions) inherited the revealed knowledge and the methodological framework established by the Prophet and practiced by the early generations. The author then notes that many jurists properly understood and applied this methodology to derive rulings for new events. Because the Tabi'un themselves had diverse opinions based on their environments, it was natural that their students and schools would also diversify. To illustrate this, the author quotes Ibn Hazm al-Zahiri, who lists prominent jurists from various regions: Abu Hanifa, Sufyan al-Thawri, and Ibn Abi Layla in Kufa; Ibn Jurayj in Mecca; Malik and Ibn al-Majishun in Medina; Uthman and Sawwar in Basra; al-Awza'i in Syria; and al-Layth in Egypt. These scholars followed the method of consulting the local Tabi'un for existing knowledge and exercising ijtihad for matters not found locally. The era is thus seen as a natural extension of the Tabi'un period, with the same events and developments continuing.

Key Terms
جدل (jadal): Dialectical reasoning as practiced in Islamic jurisprudence, though not explicitly defined in this passage. [Note: No significant shift; the term remains consistent.]
مذهب (madhhab): A school of thought or legal methodology, implied by the diversity of scholarly centers. [Note: No significant shift; stable across the tradition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Ibn Jurayj
C) Malik
D) al-Awza'i

Key Takeaway
The Mujtahid era saw the institutionalization of jadal across diverse geographical centers, each contributing to the rich tapestry of Islamic legal theory.

The Emergence of Usuli Jadal and al-Shafi'i's Intellectual Formation
Author traces the birth of usuli jadal to early usul al-fiqh and sketches al-Shafi'i's intellectual formation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why usuli jadal arose with usul al-fiqh and identify the key components of al-Shafi'i's formation that enabled him to pioneer it.

Core Idea
Usuli jadal was born from inherently controversial usul issues, and al-Shafi'i's unique education qualified him as its founder.

This passage introduces the relationship between usul al-fiqh and usuli jadal, arguing that the very nature of usul issues—being controversial—gave rise to a need for rigorous dialectical reasoning. It then sketches the remarkable intellectual formation of Imam al-Shafi'i, from his Bedouin Arabic training to his study under scholars in Mecca and Medina, showing how these experiences prepared him to author al-Risala, the first work to contain applied usuli jadal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's thesis about the origin of usuli jadal
The author claims usuli jadal emerged with the study of usul al-fiqh because usul issues are inherently controversial.
The phrase 'ma damat al-masail al-usuliyya dhatuha hiya masail khilafiyya' is key.
2
Locate the first written work containing usuli jadal
The passage explicitly states that the first work in usul al-fiqh is al-Risala by al-Shafi'i, and it contains the earliest examples of applied usuli jadal.
This is a foundational claim in the study of usuli jadal.
3
Trace al-Shafi'i's early education in language
Al-Shafi'i spent time with the Bedouin tribe of Hudhayl to learn pure Arabic and poetry, which gave him precise linguistic skills essential for nuanced argumentation.
Language mastery is crucial for jadal because many disputes hinge on word meanings.
4
Map his scholarly training in fiqh, tafsir, and hadith
He studied fiqh under Muslim ibn Khalid al-Zanji, inherited tafsir from the students of Ibn Abbas, and learned hadith from Meccan scholars, providing him with broad Islamic knowledge.
This diverse training gave him the depth needed to address usul issues.
5
Connect his formation to his later innovation
Explain how these foundations—linguistic excellence, fiqh mastery, tafsir, and hadith—enabled al-Shafi'i to integrate dialectical reasoning into usul al-fiqh in his Risala.
The combination made him uniquely qualified to pioneer usuli jadal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول: مرحلة النشأة والتأسيس. نشأ الجدل الأصولي مع نشأة الدراسة والبحث في مسائل علم أصول الفقه، إذ ما دامت المسائل الأصولية ذاتها هي مسائل خلافية، فلا شك أن حركة البحث التي انصبت عليها تكون قد استصحبت معها ذلك الخلاف ودرست تلك المسائل وهي تضع في اعتبارها الخلاف الدائر حولها. فالبحث في الجدل الأصولي - في صورته التطبيقية – إنما نشأ، أول ما نشأ، مع بدايات التأليف في علم أصول الفقه، وتحديدًا مع أول مؤلف في هذا العلم، وهو كتاب (الرسالة) للإمام محمد بن إدريس الشافعي رحمه الله. وقبل أن نتعرض إلى دراسة نماذج من صور الجدل الأصولي في هذا الكتاب، نقدم نبذة مختصرة تبرز لنا طبيعة التكوين الجدلي للإمام الشافعي، الذي أهله لأن يكون مؤسس البحث التطبيقي في الجدل الأصولي. المطلب الأول: التكوين الجدلي للإمام الشافعي. ولد أبو عبد الله محمد بن إدريس بن العباس الشافعي سنة 150 هـ بغزة أعمال عسقلان وبعد سنتين من ميلاده حملته أمه إلى موطن آبائه مكة، حيث نشأ بها يتيما، وتربى وترعرع بجوار الكعبة المشرفة في حجر أمه، فاستظهر القرآن في صباه وهو ابن سبع سنين. ثم خرج إلى هذيل بالبادية، وكانوا من أفصح العرب، فحفظ كثيرًا من أشعارهم، وقد نقل عن الشافعي قوله: إنني خرجت من مكة فلازمت هذيلا بالبادية أتعلم كلامها وآخذ طبيعتها، وكانت أفصح العرب، أرحل برحيلهم، وأنزل بنزولهم، فلما رجعت إلى مكة جعلت أنشد الأشعار، وأذكر الآداب والأخبار. وقد قال عنه الأصمعي: قرأت ديوان الهذليين على فتى من قريش يقال له محمد بن إدريس الشافعي. وحين أذن له عاد إلى مكة لزم مفتيها وفقيهها مسلم بن خالد الزنجي حتى أفتى وهو ابن خمس عشرة سنة، وقال له: أفت يا أبا عبد الله، فقد - والله - آن لك أن تفتي. وقد أخذ في مكة أيضًا تفسير القرآن الكريم عن علمائها الذين ورثوه عن ترجمان القرآن ومفسره عبد الله بن عباس رضي الله عنهما، كما تلقى أيضًا أحاديث رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من شيوخ الحديث بها، وكان حريصًا على حفظها وكتابتها. وحين بلغ العشرين من عمره، تجاوزت همته أسوار مكة لتتطلع إلى ما وراءها، إذ وصل إليه خبر إمام المدينة مالك بن أنس الله الذي اشتهر اسمه وتناقلته الركبان، قسمت همة الشافعي إلى التلقي عنه وأن يذهب إلى المدينة، خاصة بعد أن قرأ (الموطأ) الذي زادت قراءته له من رغبته في لقاء الإمام مالك رضي الله عنه. وبالفعل فقد رحل الشافعي إلى المدينة المنورة وقصد مالكا، وأخذ عنه.
First topic: The stage of emergence and establishment. Usuli jadal emerged with the emergence of study and research in the issues of the science of usul al-fiqh. Since the usuli issues themselves are controversial issues, there is no doubt that the research movement that focused on them must have accompanied that disagreement and studied those issues while taking into account the disagreement surrounding them. Thus research in usuli jadal—in its applied form—emerged, first and foremost, with the beginnings of authorship in the science of usul al-fiqh, specifically with the first work in this science, which is the book 'al-Risala' by Imam Muhammad ibn Idris al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him. Before we examine examples of forms of usuli jadal in this book, we present a brief overview highlighting the nature of the jadal formation of Imam al-Shafi'i, which qualified him to be the founder of applied research in usuli jadal. The first sub-topic: The jadal formation of Imam al-Shafi'i. Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Idris ibn al-Abbas al-Shafi'i was born in the year 150 AH in Gaza, in the districts of Ascalon. Two years after his birth, his mother took him to his ancestors' homeland, Mecca, where he grew up an orphan and was raised and nurtured next to the noble Kaaba under the care of his mother. He memorized the Quran in his childhood at the age of seven. Then he went out to the tribe of Hudhayl in the desert—they were among the most eloquent Arabs—and memorized many of their poems. It is narrated from al-Shafi'i that he said: 'I left Mecca and stayed with Hudhayl in the desert to learn their speech and adopt their nature, and they were the most eloquent Arabs. I would travel when they traveled and stay when they stayed. When I returned to Mecca, I began to recite poems and mention literature and reports.' Al-Asma'i said about him: 'I read the Diwan of the Hudhaylis to a young man from Quraysh named Muhammad ibn Idris al-Shafi'i.' When permission was given to him, he returned to Mecca and adhered to its mufti and jurist Muslim ibn Khalid al-Zanji until he gave fatwa at the age of fifteen. He said to him: 'Give fatwa, O Abu Abdullah, for by Allah it is time for you to give fatwa.' In Mecca, he also learned the interpretation of the noble Quran from its scholars who inherited it from the interpreter of the Quran and its commentator Abdullah ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both. He also received the hadiths of the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, from the hadith scholars there, and he was diligent in memorizing and writing them. When he reached twenty years of age, his ambition transcended the walls of Mecca to look beyond, as news reached him of the Imam of Medina, Malik ibn Anas, may Allah be pleased with him, whose name became famous and was carried by travelers. Al-Shafi'i's ambition divided towards receiving knowledge from him and going to Medina, especially after he read al-Muwatta', whose reading increased his desire to meet Imam Malik, may Allah be pleased with him. Indeed, al-Shafi'i traveled to Medina and sought out Malik and learned from him.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is an introductory biographical section from a work on usuli jadal. The author begins by establishing the thesis that usuli jadal emerged simultaneously with the study of usul al-fiqh itself, because the issues of usul are inherently controversial. He then specifies that the first written work in usul al-fiqh, al-Shafi'i's al-Risala, contains the earliest practical instances of usuli jadal. Before analyzing those instances, the author provides a biographical sketch to explain al-Shafi'i's unique qualifications as the founder of this field. The sketch covers al-Shafi'i's birth in Gaza in 150 AH, his early move to Mecca, orphanhood, memorization of the Quran by age seven, and his immersion in the eloquent Bedouin tribe of Hudhayl to learn pure Arabic language and poetry. The passage quotes al-Shafi'i's own description of his time with Hudhayl. It then mentions that the famous philologist al-Asma'i read the poetry of the Hudhaylis under al-Shafi'i. Upon returning to Mecca, al-Shafi'i studied under the mufti Muslim ibn Khalid al-Zanji and was authorized to give fatwa at the unusually young age of fifteen. He also studied Quranic exegesis from the inheritors of Ibn Abbas's knowledge and hadith from prominent scholars. At age twenty, his ambition drove him to travel to Medina to study under Imam Malik, especially after reading al-Muwatta'. The passage thus paints a picture of al-Shafi'i as a man steeped in Arabic language, Quran, hadith, and fiqh from diverse sources, all of which equipped him to pioneer the application of jadal in usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
الجدل الأصولي
التكوين الجدلي
الرسالة

C) Usuli jadal was developed independently of usul al-fiqh

Key Takeaway
Usuli jadal is rooted in the controversial nature of usul al-fiqh, and al-Shafi'i's unique linguistic and scholarly formation positioned him to pioneer its applied form in al-Risala.

Linguistic Foundation for Islamic Legal Reasoning
Al-Shafi'i's linguistic training among Bedouins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Imam al-Shafi'i's mastery of Arabic, acquired from Bedouins, contributed to his later legal methodology.

Core Idea
Language mastery is the first tool of a mujtahid.

Before al-Shafi'i became a master of fiqh and usul, he spent time among the Bedouins to learn pure Arabic speech and poetry. This linguistic training was not incidental; it became the foundation of his approach to interpreting the Quran and Hadith. Understanding this connection helps us see why al-Shafi'i placed such emphasis on language in his usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of al-Shafi'i's linguistic training
In the passage, al-Shafi'i states he lived among the Bedouins to learn their speech and natural disposition. This gave him direct exposure to the pure Arabic of the desert, which is considered the most authoritative for interpreting classical texts.
2
Recognize the tools he acquired
He mentions reciting poetry and learning literature and reports. These are the tools for understanding the linguistic nuances, context, and stylistic features of Arabic, essential for deriving rulings from primary sources.
3
Connect to his later methodology
Al-Shafi'i's emphasis on the Arabic language as a fundamental source of law in his Risala directly reflects this early training. He argued that legal rulings must be based on the apparent meaning of the text as understood by native Arabs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالبادية أتعلم كلامها وآخذ طبيعتها، وكانت أفصح العرب، أرحل برحيلهم، وأنزل بنزولهم، فلما رجعت إلى مكة جعلت أنشد الأشعار، وأذكر الآداب والأخبار. وقد قال عنه الأصمعي: قرأت ديوان الهذليين على فتى من قريش يقال له محمد بن إدريس الشافعي. وحين أذن له عاد إلى مكة لزم مفتيها وفقيهها مسلم بن خالد الزنجي حتى بالإفتاء وهو ابن خمس عشرة سنة، وقال له: أفت يا أبا عبد الله، فقد والله آن لك أن تفتي. وقد أخذ في مكة أيضًا تفسير القرآن الكريم عن علمائها الذين ورثوه عن ترجمان القرآن ومفسره عبد الله بن عباس رضي الله عنهما، كما تلقى أيضًا أحاديث رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من شيوخ الحديث بها، وكان حريصًا على حفظها وكتابتها. وحين بلغ العشرين من عمره، تجاوزت همته أسوار مكة لتتطلع إلى ما وراءها، إذ وصل إليه خبر إمام المدينة مالك بن أنس الذي اشتهر اسمه وتناقلته الركبان، قسمت همة الشافعي إلى التلقي عنه وأن يذهب إلى المدينة، خاصة بعد أن قرأ الموطأ الذي زادت قراءته له من رغبته في لقاء الإمام مالك رضي الله عنه. وبالفعل فقد رحل الشافعي إلى المدينة المنورة وقصد مالكا، وأخذ عنه الموطأ مشافهة، ولازمه حوالي تسع سنوات متقطعة، وكان يتفقه عليه ويدارسه في كل مسالة يستفتى فيها ويراجعه فيما يحتاج إلى المراجعة، كما اتصل بجميع علماء المدينة، وأخذ عنهم، واستفاد مما عندهم. وفي الرابعة والثلاثين من عمره، نزل الشافعي بغداد عند محمد بن الحسن فأخذ فقه العراقيين عنه، وقرأ كتبه فاجتمع له بذلك فقه الحجاز وفقه العراق، أي فقه أهل الحديث وفقه أهل الرأي، وقد قال ابن حجر في ذلك: «انتهت رياسة النقل والفقه بالمدينة إلى مالك بن أنس، فرحل إليه ولازمه وأخذ عنه، وانتهت رئاسة الفقه بالعراق إلى أبي حنيفة، فأخذ عن صاحبه محمد بن الحسن». ومع أن الشافعي أقام في بغداد ولازم محمد بن الحسن ونقل عنه حتى قال: حملت عن محمد بن الحسن وقر بعير ليس عليه إلا سماعي منه، إلا أنه كان يعتبر نفسه أصحاب مالك فقهاء مذهبه وحملة موطئه، ولذلك كان كلما خلا بتلاميذ محمد بن الحسن ناقشهم وناظرهم مدافعا عن فقه الحجازيين.
In the desert I learned their speech and took on their natural disposition, and they were the most eloquent of the Arabs. I would travel with their travels and stay with their stays. When I returned to Mecca, I began reciting poetry and mentioning literature and reports. Al-Asma'i said about him: I read the Diwan of the Hudhayl tribe to a youth from Quraysh called Muhammad ibn Idris al-Shafi'i. When he was given permission, he returned to Mecca and adhered to its mufti and jurist Muslim ibn Khalid al-Zanji until he issued fatwas while he was fifteen years old, and he said to him: Issue fatwas, O Abu Abdullah, for by Allah it is time for you to issue fatwas. He also studied in Mecca the interpretation of the Noble Quran from its scholars who inherited it from the interpreter of the Quran and its exegete Abdullah ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both. He also received the hadiths of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, from the hadith masters there, and he was keen to memorize and write them. When he reached the age of twenty, his ambition surpassed the walls of Mecca to look beyond them, for news reached him of the imam of Medina, Malik ibn Anas, whose name had become famous and was carried by travelers. Al-Shafi'i's determination was directed to receiving knowledge from him and to go to Medina, especially after reading al-Muwatta', the reading of which increased his desire to meet Imam Malik, may Allah be pleased with him. Indeed, al-Shafi'i traveled to Medina and headed to Malik, and took al-Muwatta' from him orally, and accompanied him for about nine intermittent years. He would study fiqh with him and discuss every matter on which he was asked for a fatwa, and would consult him on what needed consultation. He also connected with all the scholars of Medina, took from them, and benefited from what they had. At the age of thirty-four, al-Shafi'i settled in Baghdad with Muhammad ibn al-Hasan and took the fiqh of the Iraqis from him, and read his books. Thus he combined the fiqh of Hijaz and the fiqh of Iraq, that is, the fiqh of the people of hadith and the fiqh of the people of opinion. Ibn Hajar said regarding this: 'The leadership of transmission and fiqh in Medina ended with Malik ibn Anas, so he traveled to him, accompanied him, and took from him. And the leadership of fiqh in Iraq ended with Abu Hanifa, so he took from his companion Muhammad ibn al-Hasan.' Even though al-Shafi'i stayed in Baghdad and accompanied Muhammad ibn al-Hasan and transmitted from him until he said: 'I carried from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan a camel-load that had nothing but my hearing from him,' he nevertheless considered himself among the companions of Malik, the jurists of his school, and the carriers of his Muwatta'. Therefore, whenever he was alone with the students of Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, he would debate and argue with them in defense of the fiqh of the Hijazis.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a biographical sketch of Imam al-Shafi'i's early education and his intellectual journey. It opens with a first-person account attributed to al-Shafi'i himself, describing his time living among the Bedouins to learn their pure Arabic speech and poetry. This highlights his grounding in the Arabic language, essential for Islamic legal reasoning. The narrative then proceeds chronologically. Upon returning to Mecca, he studied under Muslim ibn Khalid al-Zanji, who recognized his maturity and authorized him to issue fatwas at the age of fifteen. This indicates the high level of trust and knowledge al-Shafi'i had already achieved. He also studied Quranic exegesis from scholars tracing back to Ibn Abbas and hadith from Meccan teachers, showing his comprehensive early training. The passage then describes his ambition to learn from Imam Malik in Medina, especially after encountering al-Muwatta'. He traveled to Medina and studied under Malik for about nine years, taking the Muwatta' orally and engaging in detailed fiqh discussions. This demonstrates his deep immersion in the Hijazi tradition of hadith and fiqh. Later, at age thirty-four, he went to Baghdad and studied under Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani, a leading Hanafi scholar. Through this, he acquired the fiqh of the Iraqis (ahl al-ra'y), thus combining both the hadith-based fiqh of Hijaz and the opinion-based fiqh of Iraq. The passage cites Ibn Hajar to emphasize that he learned from the representatives of both traditions: Malik and Abu Hanifa's student. Finally, it notes his loyalty to the Malikis, as he debated in defense of the Hijazi fiqh despite learning from the Iraqis. This background is crucial for understanding al-Shafi'i's later role in usul al-fiqh and jadal, as his synthesis of different schools informed his methodological innovations.

Synthesis of Hijazi and Iraqi Fiqh
Al-Shafi'i's study under Malik in Medina and Muhammad ibn al-Hasan in Baghdad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main legal traditions that al-Shafi'i combined and explain how this synthesis led to his independent methodology.

Core Idea
Al-Shafi'i bridged hadith-based and opinion-based fiqh to create usul al-fiqh.

The passage shows that al-Shafi'i studied under Malik in Medina (representing the hadith-focused Hijazi school) and under Muhammad ibn al-Hasan in Baghdad (representing the rationalist Iraqi school). By mastering both, he was uniquely positioned to critique and integrate them, eventually producing his own systematic approach to legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two traditions
The passage explicitly mentions 'fiqh al-Hijaz' (the fiqh of the people of hadith) and 'fiqh al-Iraq' (the fiqh of the people of opinion). Recognize that these represent two distinct methodologies.
2
Trace al-Shafi'i's exposure to each
He learned from Malik in Medina for about nine years, absorbing the Hijazi tradition. Later, he moved to Baghdad and studied under Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, a leading Hanafi scholar, acquiring the Iraqi approach.
3
Analyze the synthesis
The passage notes that al-Shafi'i, despite learning from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, remained loyal to the Maliki school and debated in its defense. But he also incorporated Iraqi methods, which later led him to formulate a middle ground: usul al-fiqh that balances textual evidence and rational analogy.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

When I returned to Mecca, I began reciting poetry and mentioning literature and reports. He also studied in Mecca the interpretation of the Noble Quran from its scholars who inherited it from the interpreter of the Quran and its exegete Abdullah ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both. He also received the hadiths of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, from the hadith masters there, and he was keen to memorize and write them. When he reached the age of twenty, his ambition surpassed the walls of Mecca to look beyond them, for news reached him of the imam of Medina, Malik ibn Anas, whose name had become famous and was carried by travelers. Al-Shafi'i's determination was directed to receiving knowledge from him and to go to Medina, especially after reading al-Muwatta', the reading of which increased his desire to meet Imam Malik, may Allah be pleased with him. Indeed, al-Shafi'i traveled to Medina and headed to Malik, and took al-Muwatta' from him orally, and accompanied him for about nine intermittent years. He would study fiqh with him and discuss every matter on which he was asked for a fatwa, and would consult him on what needed consultation. He also connected with all the scholars of Medina, took from them, and benefited from what they had. At the age of thirty-four, al-Shafi'i settled in Baghdad with Muhammad ibn al-Hasan and took the fiqh of the Iraqis from him, and read his books. Thus he combined the fiqh of Hijaz and the fiqh of Iraq, that is, the fiqh of the people of hadith and the fiqh of the people of opinion. Ibn Hajar said regarding this: 'The leadership of transmission and fiqh in Medina ended with Malik ibn Anas, so he traveled to him, accompanied him, and took from him. And the leadership of fiqh in Iraq ended with Abu Hanifa, so he took from his companion Muhammad ibn al-Hasan.' Even though al-Shafi'i stayed in Baghdad and accompanied Muhammad ibn al-Hasan and transmitted from him until he said: 'I carried from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan a camel-load that had nothing but my hearing from him,' he nevertheless considered himself among the companions of Malik, the jurists of his school, and the carriers of his Muwatta'. Therefore, whenever he was alone with the students of Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, he would debate and argue with them in defense of the fiqh of the Hijazis.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a biographical sketch of Imam al-Shafi'i's early education and his intellectual journey. It opens with a first-person account attributed to al-Shafi'i himself, describing his time living among the Bedouins to learn their pure Arabic speech and poetry. This highlights his grounding in the Arabic language, essential for Islamic legal reasoning. The narrative then proceeds chronologically. Upon returning to Mecca, he studied under Muslim ibn Khalid al-Zanji, who recognized his maturity and authorized him to issue fatwas at the age of fifteen. This indicates the high level of trust and knowledge al-Shafi'i had already achieved. He also studied Quranic exegesis from scholars tracing back to Ibn Abbas and hadith from Meccan teachers, showing his comprehensive early training. The passage then describes his ambition to learn from Imam Malik in Medina, especially after encountering al-Muwatta'. He traveled to Medina and studied under Malik for about nine years, taking the Muwatta' orally and engaging in detailed fiqh discussions. This demonstrates his deep immersion in the Hijazi tradition of hadith and fiqh. Later, at age thirty-four, he went to Baghdad and studied under Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani, a leading Hanafi scholar. Through this, he acquired the fiqh of the Iraqis (ahl al-ra'y), thus combining both the hadith-based fiqh of Hijaz and the opinion-based fiqh of Iraq. The passage cites Ibn Hajar to emphasize that he learned from the representatives of both traditions: Malik and Abu Hanifa's student. Finally, it notes his loyalty to the Malikis, as he debated in defense of the Hijazi fiqh despite learning from the Iraqis. This background is crucial for understanding al-Shafi'i's later role in usul al-fiqh and jadal, as his synthesis of different schools informed his methodological innovations.

فقه العراق

The Adab of Seeking Knowledge and Engaging in Dispute
Al-Shafi'i's travels and debates as models of scholarly adab.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify key adab (etiquettes) of seeking knowledge demonstrated by al-Shafi'i and relate them to the rules of jadal (disputation) in Islamic scholarship.

Core Idea
Al-Shafi'i's pursuit of multiple teachers exemplifies the adab of intellectual humility and thoroughness.

The passage portrays al-Shafi'i as a dedicated student who traveled widely, studied under multiple teachers, and engaged in debates with respect. His actions reflect the classical Islamic adab of seeking knowledge: reverence for teachers, critical engagement, and willingness to defend one's positions through reasoned argument. These are the same principles that underlie the discipline of jadal.

Guided Path
1
Observe al-Shafi'i's respect for his teachers
He traveled to Medina specifically to study under Malik, and later to Baghdad to learn from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan. He did not simply collect knowledge but also defended the views of his teachers (e.g., defending Hijazi fiqh).
2
Note his critical engagement
Despite learning from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, he debated his students in defense of the Hijazi school. This shows he did not blindly follow but engaged critically, a key adab of jadal.
3
Connect to the rules of jadal
In jadal, one must respect the opponent, use sound evidence, and avoid personal attacks. Al-Shafi'i's debates with the Hanafi students exemplify this: he argued using fiqh reasoning (fiqh al-Hijaz) rather than personal insult.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالبادية أتعلم كلامها وآخذ طبيعتها، وكانت أفصح العرب، أرحل برحيلهم، وأنزل بنزولهم، فلما رجعت إلى مكة جعلت أنشد الأشعار، وأذكر الآداب والأخبار. وقد قال عنه الأصمعي: قرأت ديوان الهذليين على فتى من قريش يقال له محمد بن إدريس الشافعي. وحين أذن له عاد إلى مكة لزم مفتيها وفقيهها مسلم بن خالد الزنجي حتى بالإفتاء وهو ابن خمس عشرة سنة، وقال له: أفت يا أبا عبد الله، فقد والله آن لك أن تفتي. وقد أخذ في مكة أيضًا تفسير القرآن الكريم عن علمائها الذين ورثوه عن ترجمان القرآن ومفسره عبد الله بن عباس رضي الله عنهما، كما تلقى أيضًا أحاديث رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من شيوخ الحديث بها، وكان حريصًا على حفظها وكتابتها. وحين بلغ العشرين من عمره، تجاوزت همته أسوار مكة لتتطلع إلى ما وراءها، إذ وصل إليه خبر إمام المدينة مالك بن أنس الذي اشتهر اسمه وتناقلته الركبان، قسمت همة الشافعي إلى التلقي عنه وأن يذهب إلى المدينة، خاصة بعد أن قرأ الموطأ الذي زادت قراءته له من رغبته في لقاء الإمام مالك رضي الله عنه. وبالفعل فقد رحل الشافعي إلى المدينة المنورة وقصد مالكا، وأخذ عنه الموطأ مشافهة، ولازمه حوالي تسع سنوات متقطعة، وكان يتفقه عليه ويدارسه في كل مسالة يستفتى فيها ويراجعه فيما يحتاج إلى المراجعة، كما اتصل بجميع علماء المدينة، وأخذ عنهم، واستفاد مما عندهم. وفي الرابعة والثلاثين من عمره، نزل الشافعي بغداد عند محمد بن الحسن فأخذ فقه العراقيين عنه، وقرأ كتبه فاجتمع له بذلك فقه الحجاز وفقه العراق، أي فقه أهل الحديث وفقه أهل الرأي، وقد قال ابن حجر في ذلك: «انتهت رياسة النقل والفقه بالمدينة إلى مالك بن أنس، فرحل إليه ولازمه وأخذ عنه، وانتهت رئاسة الفقه بالعراق إلى أبي حنيفة، فأخذ عن صاحبه محمد بن الحسن». ومع أن الشافعي أقام في بغداد ولازم محمد بن الحسن ونقل عنه حتى قال: حملت عن محمد بن الحسن وقر بعير ليس عليه إلا سماعي منه، إلا أنه كان يعتبر نفسه أصحاب مالك فقهاء مذهبه وحملة موطئه، ولذلك كان كلما خلا بتلاميذ محمد بن الحسن ناقشهم وناظرهم مدافعا عن فقه الحجازيين.
In the desert I learned their speech and took on their natural disposition, and they were the most eloquent of the Arabs. I would travel with their travels and stay with their stays. When I returned to Mecca, I began reciting poetry and mentioning literature and reports. Al-Asma'i said about him: I read the Diwan of the Hudhayl tribe to a youth from Quraysh called Muhammad ibn Idris al-Shafi'i. When he was given permission, he returned to Mecca and adhered to its mufti and jurist Muslim ibn Khalid al-Zanji until he issued fatwas while he was fifteen years old, and he said to him: Issue fatwas, O Abu Abdullah, for by Allah it is time for you to issue fatwas. He also studied in Mecca the interpretation of the Noble Quran from its scholars who inherited it from the interpreter of the Quran and its exegete Abdullah ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both. He also received the hadiths of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, from the hadith masters there, and he was keen to memorize and write them. He would study fiqh with him and discuss every matter on which he was asked for a fatwa, and would consult him on what needed consultation. Imam al-Shafi'i: Synthesis of Hijazi and Iraqi Fiqh
Author narrates Shafi'i's life journey, from studying with Malik to debating with Shaybani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the intellectual influences that shaped Imam al-Shafi'i's legal methodology and identify the key figures and regions that contributed to his synthesis.

Core Idea
Al-Shafi'i combined the hadith-based fiqh of Hijaz with the ra'y-based fiqh of Iraq.

This biography introduces us to the intellectual journey of Imam al-Shafi'i, the founder of the Shafi'i school of jurisprudence. Understanding his education under Malik in Madina and Muhammad al-Shaybani in Baghdad helps us see how he later developed his own legal theory that balanced revelation and reason. This context is essential for appreciating his contributions to usul al-fiqh and the dialectical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Shafi'i's primary teachers
Locate the two main scholars al-Shafi'i studied under: Malik ibn Anas in Madina (representing Hijazi fiqh) and Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani in Baghdad (representing Iraqi fiqh). Note the duration and nature of his studies with each.
Malik taught him the Muwatta and the Hijazi method; Muhammad ibn al-Hasan taught him the Hanafi method.
2
Trace the geographic sequence of his travels
List the key cities al-Shafi'i lived in and the periods: Madina (childhood/youth), Baghdad (first stay at age 34), Makkah (after return, taught for 9 years), Baghdad (second stay, year 195 AH), Egypt (until death in 204 AH). Understand why he moved each time.
His second departure from Baghdad was due to political and theological conditions (Mu'tazilite influence under al-Ma'mun).
3
Analyze his relationship with the Hanafi school
Notice that while al-Shafi'i deeply studied with Muhammad ibn al-Hasan and even carried his books, he remained loyal to the Maliki school initially. He debated Muhammad's students, defending Hijazi fiqh, but avoided debating the imam himself out of respect.
This shows his scholarly independence and eventual synthesis.
4
Extract the intellectual synthesis
From Ibn Hajar's quote, understand that al-Shafi'i combined the 'fiqh of hadith' (Hijaz) with the 'fiqh of ra'y' (Iraq). This synthesis formed the basis of his own school, which sought to mediate between textualism and rationalism.
Al-Shafi'i's own usul al-fiqh (e.g., al-Risala) reflects this balance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموطأ مشافهة، ولازمه حوالي تسع سنوات متقطعة، وكان يتفقه عليه ويدارسه في كل مسألة يستفتى فيها ويراجعه فيما يحتاج إلى المراجعة، كما اتصل بجميع علماء المدينة، وأخذ عنهم، واستفاد مما عندهم.

وفي الرابعة والثلاثين من عمره، نزل الشافعي بغداد عند محمد بن الحسن فأخذ فقه العراقيين عنه، وقرأ كتبه فاجتمع له بذلك فقه الحجاز وفقه العراق، أي فقه أهل الحديث وفقه أهل الرأي، وقد قال ابن حجر في ذلك: «انتهت رياسة النقل والفقه بالمدينة إلى مالك بن أنس، فرحل إليه ولازمه وأخذ عنه، وانتهت رئاسة الفقه بالعراق إلى أبي حنيفة، فأخذ عن صاحبه محمد بن الحسن».

ومع أن الشافعي أقام في بغداد ولازم محمد بن الحسن ونقل عنه حتى قال: حملت عن محمد بن الحسن وقر بعير ليس عليه إلا سماعي منه، إلا أنه كان يعتبر نفسه أصحاب مالك فقهاء مذهبه وحملة موطئه، ولذلك كان كلما خلا بتلاميذ محمد بن الحسن ناقشهم وناظرهم مدافعا عن فقه الحجازيين وطريقتهم، وكان - مع ذلك - يتفادى مناظرة الإمام محمد نفسه في أول الأمر، وذلك احترامًا منه وتعظيمًا لمكانة أستاذه حتى بلغ محمدًا الخبر فطلب إليه أن يناظره فامتنع ولكن الإمام محمد ألح عليه في ذلك فخضع لطلبه وناظره.

عاد الشافعي من العراق إلى مكة، فأخذ يلقي دروسه في الحرم المكي ويلتقي بكبار العلماء في موسم الحج، وكان ممن التقى بهم أحمد بن حنبل، وقد ظل في إلقاء دروسه بمكة مدة طويلة ذكر الرواة أنها بلغت تسع سنين ظهرت فيها شخصيته في نضجه الفكري ومنهجه العلمي والفقهي.

ثم سافر إلى بغداد للمرة الثانية سنة 195هـ، ولكن لم يطب له المقام فيها لغلبة العنصر الفارسي في عهد المأمون وما كان من تقريبه المعتزلة وميله إلى مناهج بحثهم، وقد دعي إلى مصر، فاستجاب للدعوة ورحل إليها، فنشر بها علمه وآراءه وفقهه حتى مات في آخر رجب سنة 204هـ، وقد بلغ من العمر أربعًا وخمسين سنة.

كان رحمه الله علما بين العلماء، لا يجارى في علمه؛ يقول فيه الإمام أحمد ابن حنبل: يروى عن النبي أن الله عز وجل يبعث لهذه الأمة على رأس كل مائة سنة رجلا يقيم لها أمر دينها، فكان عمر بن عبد العزيز على رأس المائة، وأرجو أن يكون الشافعي على رأس المائة الأخرى.
He studied the Muwatta orally and accompanied him for about nine intermittent years, and he would study fiqh with him and review with him every issue in which he was asked for a legal opinion and would consult him regarding what needed consultation. He also connected with all the scholars of Madinah, took from them, and benefited from what they had.

At the age of thirty-four, al-Shafi'i settled in Baghdad with Muhammad ibn al-Hasan and learned the fiqh of the Iraqis from him, and read his books, thus combining for himself the fiqh of the Hijaz and the fiqh of Iraq, that is, the fiqh of the people of hadith and the fiqh of the people of opinion. Ibn Hajar said about this: 'The leadership of transmission and fiqh in Madinah ended with Malik ibn Anas, so he traveled to him, accompanied him, and took from him. And the leadership of fiqh in Iraq ended with Abu Hanifah, so he took from his companion Muhammad ibn al-Hasan.'

Although al-Shafi'i stayed in Baghdad and accompanied Muhammad ibn al-Hasan and transmitted from him until he said: 'I carried from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan a camel-load, nothing on it except my audition from him,' he nevertheless considered himself one of Malik's companions, the jurists of his school, and the carriers of his Muwatta. Therefore, whenever he was alone with Muhammad ibn al-Hasan's students, he would debate and argue with them, defending the fiqh of the Hijazis and their way. Yet, at first, he avoided debating Imam Muhammad himself out of respect and veneration for his teacher's status, until the news reached Muhammad, who asked him to debate. He refused, but Imam Muhammad insisted, so he complied with his request and debated him.

Al-Shafi'i returned from Iraq to Makkah and began delivering his lessons in the Sacred Mosque and meeting senior scholars during the Hajj season. Among those he met was Ahmad ibn Hanbal. He continued delivering his lessons in Makkah for a long period, which narrators said reached nine years, during which his personality emerged in his intellectual maturity and his scholarly and juristic methodology.

Then he traveled to Baghdad for the second time in the year 195 AH, but he did not find staying there pleasant due to the dominance of the Persian element during the reign of al-Ma'mun and his favoring of the Mu'tazilites and his inclination toward their methods of inquiry. He was invited to Egypt, so he accepted the invitation and traveled there, and he spread his knowledge, opinions, and fiqh there until he died at the end of Rajab in the year 204 AH, at the age of fifty-four.

He was, may Allah have mercy on him, a banner among scholars, unrivaled in his knowledge. Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal said about him: 'It is narrated from the Prophet that Allah Almighty sends for this community at the head of every hundred years a person who revives for it its religion. So Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz was at the head of the first century, and I hope that al-Shafi'i is at the head of the next century.'
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of Imam al-Shafi'i's life, focusing on his education and intellectual development. It begins with his study of Malik's Muwatta orally over nine years and his engagement with all the scholars of Madinah. Then it describes how at age 34 he moved to Baghdad and studied under Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani, the companion of Abu Hanifah, thereby combining the fiqh of the Hijaz (represented by Malik) and the fiqh of Iraq (represented by Abu Hanifah). Ibn Hajar's quote underscores that the leadership of the two regions culminated in these two imams. The passage then highlights al-Shafi'i's loyalty to the Maliki school: despite his deep study with Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, he considered himself a carrier of the Muwatta and would debate Muhammad's students in defense of the Hijazi way, though he initially avoided debating Muhammad himself out of respect. After returning to Makkah, he taught for nine years and met Ahmad ibn Hanbal. His second trip to Baghdad was cut short due to political and theological turmoil (the rise of Mu'tazilism under al-Ma'mun), so he moved to Egypt, where he spread his own school and died at 54. The passage ends with Ahmad ibn Hanbal's praise, expressing hope that al-Shafi'i is the mujaddid (renewer) of the second century, echoing a hadith about a renewer every century. This biography provides context for understanding al-Shafi'i's role in the development of Islamic dialectic (jadal) and usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which two geographical regions did al-Shafi'i combine in his legal education according to the passage?
A) Syria and Iraq
B) Hijaz and Iraq
C) Egypt and Hijaz
D) Iraq and Persia

Key Takeaway
Al-Shafi'i's intellectual journey from Madina to Baghdad to Egypt allowed him to synthesize the two major legal traditions of his time, leading to the formation of his own independent school of fiqh.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
